Salah — Prayer

6 min
Speech as Response: The 'Yarhamukallah' Rule in Prayer
Establishes a qawida and applies it to the sneeze response in salah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qawida that speech in response context is treated as a response, and apply it to determine that saying 'yarhamukallah' in prayer invalidates it.

Core Idea
Speech in prayer that is a common social response invalidates, even if it could be a du'a.

This lesson examines a key principle from Al-Hidayah: when speech is placed in the context of a response and can bear that meaning, it is legally considered a response. The prime application is the sneeze response 'yarhamukallah' during prayer. Even though the phrase is a du'a, its use as a response makes it kalam al-nas, thus invalidating the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage states the qawida: speech uttered in response context and capable of being a response is treated as a response.
2
Examine the application
The example: responding to a sneeze with 'yarhamukallah' in prayer. Because it is a common social response, it is kalam al-nas.
3
Compare with other phrases
The analogy is made to saying 'May Allah prolong your life' – also a common response, not a prayer du'a in this context.
This reinforces that the context determines the classification.
4
Derive the ruling
Therefore, saying 'yarhamukallah' in prayer as a response invalidates the prayer (batil).
5
Consider the underlying illa
The effective cause is the context of response and common usage, not the literal meaning of the phrase.
This is a key point for distinguishing similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٠٠) إِنَّ الْكَلَامَ إِذَا أَخْرَجَ مَخْرَجَ الْجَوَابِ وَهُوَ يَحْتَمِلُهُ يُجْعَلُ جَوَابًا. ترجمہ: جب کلام جواب کے طور پر استعمال کیا جائے اور وہ کلام جواب کا احتمال بھی رکھتا ہو تو اس کو جواب قرار دیا جائے گا۔ وَجَوَابُ عَاطِسٍ بِيَرْحَمُكَ اللَّهُ لِأَنَّهُ يَجْرِى فِي مُخَاطَبَاتِ النَّاسِ فَصَارَ كَمَا لَوْ قَالَ أَطَالَ اللَّهُ بَقَاءِ كَ فَكَانَ مِنْ كَلَامِهِم.
(100) Indeed, when speech is put in the context of a response and it can bear that meaning, it is considered a response. Translation: When speech is used as a response and it can also bear the meaning of a response, it will be considered a response. And the response to the sneezer with 'yarhamukallah' [is an example] because it occurs in people's conversations, so it is as if he said 'May Allah prolong your life' and it was from their speech.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents qawida (100) from al-Hidayah: if a phrase is uttered in a context that makes it a response, and the phrase itself is capable of being a response, then it is legally treated as a response. The commentary applies this to the sneeze response 'yarhamukallah' once again, noting that even if one argues it is a du'a, because it is uttered as a response to a sneeze in common usage, it is considered a response and thus kalam al-nas. The analogy is made to saying 'May Allah prolong your life' as a response – both are common social responses. Therefore, saying 'yarhamukallah' during prayer as a response invalidates it, reinforcing the earlier principle.

Key Terms
جواب (jawab): A response; speech that is said in reply to something and bears that meaning.
مخرج جواب (makhraj al-jawab): The context or manner of utterance that indicates it is a response.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle in this lesson, if a person in prayer says 'yarhamukallah' as a response to a sneeze, how is it treated?
A) It is treated as a du'a and does not invalidate.
B) It is treated as a response and invalidates prayer.
C) It is treated as a response but still does not invalidate.
D) It depends on whether the sneezer is also in prayer.

Key Takeaway
Speech in prayer that is a common social response is kalam al-nas and invalidates, regardless of its literal meaning.

Salah — Prayer

Speech as Response Invalidates Prayer: The Maxim Applied
States a maxim and applies it to a prayer invalidation scenario.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on responsive speech, apply it to determine whether a phrase in prayer is kalam al-nas, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Intent turns dhikr into conversation: speech used as a response is ruled a response, even if it could be praise.

This lesson examines a critical legal maxim from Hanafi fiqh: when speech is uttered as a response and can bear that meaning, it is legally treated as a response. The rule has direct implications for prayer: if a worshipper answers someone's question with 'La ilaha illa Allah', the prayer becomes invalid according to the majority of Hanafis, because the intention to respond transforms the phrase into human speech (kalam al-nas). We will unpack the maxim, the intra-madhhab dispute with Abu Yusuf, and the consensus cases that solidify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi maxim and its scope
Identify the maxim from the text: 'When speech is used as a response and it can bear that meaning, it is considered a response.' Note that it applies to any phrase that could be either dhikr or a response.
The maxim is a qa'ida sughra (minor rule) governing the classification of speech in prayer.
2
Identify the main application: answering in prayer
Consider the scenario: a person praying is asked 'Is there any god besides Allah?' and responds 'La ilaha illa Allah'. Determine the ruling according to the maxim: the speech is used as a response, so it is ruled a response, i.e., kalam al-nas, which invalidates prayer.
The phrase itself could be dhikr, but the intention to respond overrides that.
3
Layout the intra-Hanafi dispute
Record the positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it invalidates; Abu Yusuf says it does not because the phrase is inherently praise (thana'). Understand each imam's reasoning.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the same school.
4
Follow the proofs for each side
Analyze the proofs: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rely on the maxim and the analogy (qiyas) to responding to a sneeze with 'Yarhamukallah', which also invalidates prayer. Abu Yusuf argues that the words are thana' in their wording, and intention does not change their inherent nature as dhikr.
The analogy to sneezing is a qiyas that shows the principle is consistent.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and thamarah
Identify that the mashayikh (later scholars) have specified certain phrases that invalidate by agreement (e.g., reciting Quranic verses as a direct address), which supports the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Conclude that the relied-upon position is that such responses invalidate prayer.
The thamarah al-khilaf: according to Abu Yusuf, the prayer is valid; according to the majority, it is invalid and must be repeated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٠٠) إِنَّ الْكَلَامَ إِذَا أَخْرَجَ مَخْرَجَ الْجَوَابِ وَهُوَ يَحْتَمِلُهُ يُجْعَلُ جَوَابًا. ترجمہ: جب کلام جواب کے طور پر استعمال کیا جائے اور وہ کلام جواب کا احتمال بھی رکھتا ہو تو اس کو جواب قرار دیا جائے گا۔ وَجَوَابُ عَاطِسٍ بِيَرْحَمُكَ اللَّهُ لِأَنَّهُ يَجْرِى فِي مُخَاطَبَاتِ النَّاسِ فَصَارَ كَمَا لَوْ قَالَ أَطَالَ اللَّهُ بَقَاءِ كَ فَكَانَ مِنْ كَلَامِهِمْ. تبيين الحقائق كتاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة، ج ۱ ص ۱۵۶، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي] الهداية كاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها ، ج ١ ص ١٣٧ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ہر ایسا کلام جو بطور جواب کے استعمال کیا جائے اور وہ کلام جواب بننے کا احتمال بھی رکھتا ہو تو اس کلام کو جواب قرار دیا جائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے مذکورہ اصول ذکر کرنے کے بعد اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر ایک آدمی نماز پڑھ رہا ہو اور باہر سے ایک آدمی آیا اور اس نے مصلی سے کہا هَلْ مِنْ إِلَهِ غَيْرُ اللَّهِ “ کیا اللہ کے سوا کوئی معبود ہے؟ تو اس مصلی نے جواب میں کہا لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ “ تو عند الطرفين اس مصلی کی نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی ، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ “ یہ ایسا کلام ہے جو، جواب کے طور پر بھی استعمال ہوتا ہے اور حمد باری تعالی کے لئے بھی لیکن جب مصلی نے اس کو جواب کے طور پر استعمال کیا تو اس کا ایک معنی متعین کر دیا ، لہذا امصلی کے اس کلام کو جواب قرار دیا جائے گا اور مذکورہ اصول کی بنیاد پر یہ کلام مفسد صلاۃ ہوگا، لہذامصلی کی نماز اس سے فاسد ہو جائے گی۔ (قَوْلُهُ وَالْجَوَابُ بِلا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ أَى يُفْسِدُهَا عِندَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ لَا يَكُونُ مُفْسِدًا لِأَنَّهُ ثَنَاءٌ بِصِيغَتِهِ فَلَا يَتَغَيَّرُ بِعَزِيمَتِهِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّهُ أَخْرَجَ الْكَلَامَ مَخْرَجَ الْجَوَابِ وَهُوَ يَحْتَمِلُهُ فَيُجْعَلُ جَوَابًا كَتَشْمِيتِ الْعَاطِسِ وَلَيْسَ مَقْصُودُ الْمُصَنِّفِ خُصُوصَ الْجَوَابِ بِهَذِهِ الْكَلِمَةِ بَلْ كُلُّ كَلِمَةٍ هِيَ ذِكْرٌ أَوْ قُرْآنٌ قَصَدَ بِهَا الْجَوَابَ فَهِيَ عَلَى الْخِلَافِ كَمَا إِذَا أُخْبِرَ بِخَبَرٍ يَسُرُّهُ فَقَالَ الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ أَوْ بِأَمْرٍ عَجِيبٍ فَقَالَ سُبْحَانَ اللَّهِ ثُمَّ نَصَّ الْمَشَايِخُ عَلَى أَشْيَاءَ مُوجِبَةٍ لِلْفَسَادِ بِاتِّفَاقِهِمْ وَهُوَ مَا لَوْ كَانَ بَيْنَ يَدَى الْمُصَلَّى كَابٌ مَوْضُوعٌ وَعِنْدَهُ رَجُلٌ اسْمُهُ يَحْيَى فَقَالَ يَا يَحْيَى خُذِ الْكِتَابَ بِقُوَّةٍ أَوْ رَجُلٌ اسْمُهُ مُوسَى وَبِيَدِهِ عَمَّا فَقَالَ لَهُ (وَمَا تِلْكَ بِيَمِينِكَ يَا مُوسَى أَوْ كَانَ فِى السَّفِينَةِ وَابْنُهُ خَارِجَهَا فَقَالَ يَا بُنَيَّ ارْكَبُ مَعَنَا أَوْ طُرِقَ عَلَيْهِ الْبَابُ أَوْ نُودِيَ مِنْ خَارِجهِ فَقَالَ (وَمَنْ دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا، وَأَرَادَ بِهَذِهِ الْأَلْفَاظَ الْخِطَابَ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُشْكِلُ عَلَى أَحَدٍ أَنَّهُ مُتَكَلَّمْ لا قَارِيِّ وَهِيَ مُؤَيَّدَةٌ لِمَا قَالَاهُ. البحر الرائق كاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة، ج ۲ ص ۷، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي] الهداية كاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة، فصل، ج ۱ ص ۱۴۶، ط: رحمانیہ
100. Indeed, when speech is used as a response and it can bear that meaning, it is considered a response. Translation: When speech is used as a response and that speech has the potential to be a response, it will be considered a response. And the response to a sneezer with 'Yarhamukallah' because it occurs in people's addresses, so it becomes as if he said 'Atala Allahu baqa'ika' and it was from their speech. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that every speech that is used as a response and has the possibility of being a response will be considered a response. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, is establishing that if a person is praying and another person comes from outside and asks the prayer performer 'Is there any god besides Allah?' and the prayer performer responds with 'La ilaha illa Allah', then according to both positions the prayer becomes invalid. The reason is that 'La ilaha illa Allah' is speech that is used both as a response and as praise of Allah, but when the prayer performer used it as a response, he determined one meaning. Therefore, his speech will be considered a response, and based on the principle, this speech invalidates prayer. His statement: And the answer with 'La ilaha illa Allah' means it invalidates according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It does not invalidate because it is praise in its wording, so it does not change by his intention. For them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), he used the speech as a response, and it can bear it, so it becomes a response like responding to a sneezer. And the author's intent is not specific to this word; rather every word that is dhikr or Quran, if he intends it as a response, it is subject to the same disagreement. For example, if he is informed of joyful news and says 'Alhamdulillah' or a wondrous event and says 'Subhanallah'. Then the mashayikh have specified certain things that invalidate by agreement, such as if there is a book placed before the prayer performer and a man named Yahya, and he says 'O Yahya, take the book with strength', or a man named Musa with a staff in his hand, and he says to him 'And what is that in your right hand, O Musa?', or if he is in a ship and his son is outside, and he says 'O my son, embark with us', or if someone knocks on the door or calls from outside, and he says 'And whoever enters it is secure', intending address with these words, because it is not problematic for anyone that he is speaking, not reciting. And this supports what they (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) said.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a second legal maxim: if speech is used in the context of a response, and it can bear that meaning, it is legally treated as a response. The author applies this to a person in prayer who, when asked 'Is there any god besides Allah?', responds with 'La ilaha illa Allah'. Although this phrase could be dhikr, the intention to respond makes it kalam al-nas, which invalidates prayer. The text then details an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that it invalidates, while Abu Yusuf argues it does not because the phrase is praise regardless of intent. The response of the two is that the intent to respond changes its ruling, analogous to responding to a sneeze. The mashayikh have enumerated cases where there is consensus on invalidation, supporting the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
جواب (jawab): A response; speech intended to answer another person's statement or question.
ثناء (thana'): Praise; speech that glorifies Allah, such as 'Alhamdulillah' or 'La ilaha illa Allah'.
تشمت العاطس (tashmit al-'atis): Responding to a sneezer with 'Yarhamukallah'; an example of speech that is a response even if it is a du'a.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, why does saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' in response to a question during prayer invalidate the prayer?
A) Because it is a lie.
B) Because the intention makes it a response, and responding is kalam al-nas.
C) Because it is too long.
D) Because it is forbidden to speak in prayer.

Key Takeaway
Speech uttered as a response in prayer, even if it could be dhikr, invalidates the prayer; the intention to respond is decisive.

The Roof of a Mosque Has the Same Ruling as the Mosque
Introduces a maxim about the legal status of a mosque's roof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the ruling that the roof of a mosque has the same hukm as the mosque and APPLY it to at least two specific rulings (e.g., prohibition for junub, i'tikaf).

Core Idea
The roof of a mosque is legally part of the mosque itself.

This short maxim from Al-Hidayah is a foundational spatial principle in Hanafi fiqh. It clarifies that the roof (sath) of a mosque is not a separate neutral area; rather, it inherits all the legal rulings of the mosque interior. This simplifies many questions about what is permitted or prohibited on the roof, from entering in a state of janabah to performing i'tikaf there.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Maxim
Read the Arabic maxim and its translation. Note the term 'sath al-masjid' and 'hukm al-masjid'. The maxim is a statement of legal identity: the roof shares the same rulings as the mosque building and courtyard.
The maxim is in the form of a khabari (declarative) sentence, indicating a binding legal equivalence.
2
Identify the Implications
Think about what rulings apply to a mosque: prohibitions (janabah, menstruating person staying), obligations (respect, removal of shoes), and worship rules (i'tikaf conditions, prayer validity). Apply each of these to the roof.
The roof includes any covered surface on top of the mosque structure, including flat roofs used for prayer.
3
Apply to a Scenario
Consider a concrete case: A person in a state of janabah goes onto the mosque roof to retrieve a forgotten item. Using the maxim, determine that they must not linger on the roof, just as they would not linger inside the mosque.
The maxim also applies to extensions and attached structures that are considered part of the mosque.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ١٠١) إِنَّ سَطْحَ الْمَسْجِدِ لَهُ حُكْمُ الْمَسْجِدِ . )
101. Indeed, the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is a brief legal maxim stating that the roof (sath) of a mosque is legally equivalent to the mosque itself. This means that rulings applicable to the mosque — such as prohibition for junub to stay, rules of i'tikaf, removal of shoes, etc. — also apply to the roof. The principle is foundational for understanding spatial rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
سطح المسجد (sath al-masjid): The roof or upper surface of a mosque.
حكم المسجد (hukm al-masjid): The legal rulings that apply to a mosque, e.g., prohibition of janabah, rules of i'tikaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if a person in janabah enters the mosque roof, what ruling applies?
A) It is allowed because it is not the prayer hall.
B) It is prohibited for them to stay, just as inside the mosque.
C) It is allowed only if they have wudu.
D) It is recommended to leave immediately but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The roof of a mosque is legally identical to the mosque; all its rulings apply to the roof.

The Principle That Response Speech Invalidates Prayer: The Case of 'La ilaha illallah'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on response speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle governing speech as a response in prayer and state the intra-Hanafi dispute on saying 'La ilaha illallah'.

Core Idea
Speech intended as a response in prayer invalidates it, even if it is dhikr, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does a worshipper's utterance of 'La ilaha illallah' as a response to a question invalidate the prayer? The passage from Al-Hidayah and related commentaries lays down a general principle: any speech that can be and is used as a response is treated as a response. The lesson will walk through the dispute, the proofs, and the consensus cases.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
The author first establishes that if a worshipper uses speech that can be a response as a response, it is considered a response and invalidates the prayer.
This principle is key to understanding the dispute.
2
Apply the principle to the example
See how the principle is applied to the case of 'La ilaha illallah' in response to the question 'Hal min ilahin ghayr Allah'.
The words are capable of being a response and are used as such.
3
Identify the dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it invalidates; Abu Yusuf disagrees because the words are praise by form.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the proofs
For Abu Hanifa: intention to respond overrides form; analogy to sneeze response. For Abu Yusuf: form of thana' remains unchanged.
Each proof relies on a different aspect of the speech.
5
Note the consensus cases
Later shuyukh agree on certain cases like addressing by name with Quranic verses are unanimously invalidating.
These cases support the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ہر ایسا کلام جو بطور جواب کے استعمال کیا جائے اور وہ کلام جواب بننے کا احتمال بھی رکھتا ہو تو اس کلام کو جواب قرار دیا جائے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے مذکورہ اصول ذکر کرنے کے بعد اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر ایک آدمی نماز پڑھ رہا ہو اور باہر سے ایک آدمی آیا اور اس نے مصلی سے کہا هَلْ مِنْ إِلَهِ غَيْرُ اللَّهِ “ کیا اللہ کے سوا کوئی معبود ہے؟ تو اس مصلی نے جواب میں کہا لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ “ تو عند الطرفين اس مصلی کی نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی ، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ “ یہ ایسا کلام ہے جو، جواب کے طور پر بھی استعمال ہوتا ہے اور حمد باری تعالی کے لئے بھی لیکن جب مصلی نے اس کو جواب کے طور پر استعمال کیا تو اس کا ایک معنی متعین کر دیا ، لہذا امصلی کے اس کلام کو جواب قرار دیا جائے گا اور مذکورہ اصول کی بنیاد پر یہ کلام مفسد صلاۃ ہوگا، لہذامصلی کی نماز اس سے فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
(قَوْلُهُ وَالْجَوَابُ بِلا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ أَى يُفْسِدُهَا عِندَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ لَا يَكُونُ مُفْسِدًا لِأَنَّهُ ثَنَاءٌ بِصِيغَتِهِ فَلَا يَتَغَيَّرُ بِعَزِيمَتِهِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّهُ أَخْرَجَ الْكَلَامَ مَخْرَجَ الْجَوَابِ وَهُوَ يَحْتَمِلُهُ فَيُجْعَلُ جَوَابًا كَتَشْمِيتِ الْعَاطِسِ وَلَيْسَ مَقْصُودُ الْمُصَنِّفِ خُصُوصَ الْجَوَابِ بِهَذِهِ الْكَلِمَةِ بَلْ كُلُّ كَلِمَةٍ هِيَ ذِكْرٌ أَوْ قُرْآنٌ قَصَدَ بِهَا الْجَوَابَ فَهِيَ عَلَى الْخِلَافِ كَمَا إِذَا أُخْبِرَ بِخَبَرٍ يَسُرُّهُ فَقَالَ الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ أَوْ بِأَمْرٍ عَجِيبٍ فَقَالَ سُبْحَانَ اللَّهِ ثُمَّ نَصَّ الْمَشَايِخُ عَلَى أَشْيَاءَ مُوجِبَةٍ لِلْفَسَادِ بِاتِّفَاقِهِمْ وَهُوَ مَا لَوْ كَانَ بَيْنَ يَدَى الْمُصَلَّى كَابٌ مَوْضُوعٌ وَعِنْدَهُ رَجُلٌ اسْمُهُ يَحْيَى فَقَالَ يَا يَحْيَى خُذِ الْكِتَابَ بِقُوَّةٍ أَوْ رَجُلٌ اسْمُهُ مُوسَى وَبِيَدِهِ عَمَّا فَقَالَ لَهُ (وَمَا تِلْكَ بِيَمِينِكَ يَا مُوسَى أَوْ كَانَ فِى السَّفِينَةِ وَابْنُهُ خَارِجَهَا فَقَالَ يَا بُنَيَّ ارْكَبُ مَعَنَا أَوْ طُرِقَ عَلَيْهِ الْبَابُ أَوْ نُودِيَ مِنْ خَارِجهِ فَقَالَ (وَمَنْ دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا، وَأَرَادَ بِهَذِهِ الْأَلْفَاظَ الْخِطَابَ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُشْكِلُ عَلَى أَحَدٍ أَنَّهُ مُتَكَلَّمْ لا قَارِيِّ وَهِيَ مُؤَيَّدَةٌ لِمَا قَالَاهُ.
البحر الرائق كاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة، ج ۲ ص ۷، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي]
الهداية كاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة، فصل، ج ۱ ص ۱۴۶، ط: رحمانیہ
Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that every speech that is used as a response and that speech has the possibility of being a response, then that speech will be considered a response. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the mentioned principle, is proving that if a person is praying and a man comes from outside and says to the worshipper, 'Is there any god other than Allah?' Then the worshipper replies, 'There is no god but Allah,' then according to both sides the worshipper's prayer becomes invalid. The reason is that 'La ilaha illallah' is speech that is used as a response and also for praise of Allah, but when the worshipper used it as a response, he determined its meaning. Therefore, his speech will be considered a response and based on the mentioned principle this speech will be a nullifier of prayer, so the worshipper's prayer becomes invalid. (His saying: And the answer with 'La ilaha illallah' means it invalidates it according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf said it does not invalidate because it is praise in its form, so it does not change by his intention. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that he brought the speech out as a response and it is capable of it, so it is made a response like replying to a sneeze. And the author's intent is not specifically the answer with this word, rather every word that is remembrance or Quran, if he intends by it the answer, then it is subject to the dispute. As if he is informed of news that pleases him and he says 'Alhamdulillah', or of an amazing matter and he says 'Subhanallah'. Then the shuyukh have stipulated things that cause invalidation by consensus, such as if there is a placed book before the worshipper and there is a man named Yahya, and he says, 'O Yahya, take the book with strength', or a man named Musa and in his hand is a staff, and he says to him, 'And what is that in your right hand, O Musa?', or he was on a ship and his son is outside it, and he says, 'O my son, embark with us', or the door is knocked upon him or he is called from outside and he says, 'And whoever enters it is safe', and he intends by these words address because it is not ambiguous to anyone that he is a speaker not a reciter, and they support what they (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) said. [Al-Bahr al-Raiq, Kitab al-Salah, Bab ma yufsid al-salah, vol. 2 p. 7, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami] [Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Salah, Bab ma yufsid al-salah, Fasl, vol. 1 p. 146, Rahmaniyya]
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on a worshipper in prayer who responds to a question with the words 'La ilaha illallah'. The author first explains a general usul (principle): any speech that can be used as a response and is actually used as a response will be treated as a response. He then applies this to the example: if a person outside the prayer asks 'Is there any god other than Allah?' and the worshipper replies 'La ilaha illallah', this invalidates the prayer because the intention of the worshipper was to answer, not to recite dhikr. The Arabic commentary from the Hidayah elaborates: According to Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, this invalidates the prayer. Abu Yusuf argues it does not, because the words are inherently praise (thana') and their form as praise does not change by the worshipper's intention. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that the worshipper deliberately used the words as a response, and since the words can bear that meaning, they are treated as a response, analogous to responding to a sneeze with 'Yarhamukallah' (which also invalidates prayer). The principle is general: any word of dhikr or Quran intended as a response is subject to the same dispute. The passage then notes that the shuyukh (later scholars) have identified specific cases where all agree the prayer is invalidated, such as addressing a person named Yahya with the Quranic verse 'O Yahya, take the book with strength', or similar examples where the worshipper clearly intends to speak to someone, not recite. These cases are unanimously invalid and support the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Thus, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that responding with such words invalidates the prayer.

Key Terms
الجواب (al-jawab): In this context, speech that is used to respond to a question or address, as opposed to being recited as dhikr or Quran.
ثناء (thana'): Praise (of Allah), which in its original form is permissible in prayer and not a nullifier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following scenarios would invalidate the prayer according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) A worshipper recites 'La ilaha illallah' as part of dhikr without external prompt.
B) A worshipper says 'Subhanallah' in reaction to a loud noise without intending to respond.
C) A worshipper says 'La ilaha illallah' in reply to someone asking 'Who is your Lord?' intending to answer.
D) A worshipper says 'Alhamdulillah' after sneezing inside prayer.

Key Takeaway
The intention to respond overrides the form of dhikr, and speech capable of being a response invalidates prayer if used as such, per the relied-upon Hanafi position.

The Roof of a Mosque Shares Its Rulings
States and explains the legal principle that the mosque roof shares its rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that the mosque roof has the same ruling as the mosque and apply it to determine the permissibility of actions on the roof.

Core Idea
The mosque roof is legally identical to the mosque interior for all rulings.

Have you ever wondered whether the roof of a mosque is treated legally like the mosque itself? The Hanafi school answers with a clear principle: the roof shares the same rulings as the mosque. This lesson unpacks that principle and its practical applications, from following the imam in prayer to prohibitions that apply even on the rooftop.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
The passage opens with the foundational statement: 'Inna satha al-masjidi lahu hukmu al-masjid' (Indeed, the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque). Commit this phrasing to memory.
2
Trace the Authority
The principle is attributed to Imam Muhammad and is found in Al-Muhit al-Burhani and Al-Bahr al-Raiq. Note that it is from the zahir al-riwayah, meaning it is well-established.
3
Apply to Prohibited Acts
Intercourse, urination, and defecation are prohibited on the roof because they are prohibited inside the mosque. The text specifies that the 'dislike' (karahah) is of the tahrimi type (prohibited dislike, close to haram).
4
Apply to Permissible Acts
Following the imam from the roof is valid, and climbing onto the roof does not break i'tikaf. Conversely, a junub, menstruating woman, or woman with postpartum bleeding may not stay on the roof.
5
Understand the Level of Prohibition
The ruling is makruh tahrimi, not haram. Hanafis distinguish this as a sinful act not proven by a definitive text. The text explicitly clarifies 'al-murad bi al-karahah karahah al-tahrim'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ سَطْحَ الْمَسْجِدِ لَهُ حُكْمُ الْمَسْجِدِ .
ترجمہ: مسجد کی چھت کے لئے مسجد کا حکم ہے۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو حکم مسجد کا ہے وہی حکم مسجد کی چھت کا بھی ہے، یعنی جو کام مسجد میں جائز ہے وہی مسجد کی چھت پر جائز ہے، اور جو کام مسجد میں جائز نہیں ہے وہ کام مسجد کی چھت پر کرنا بھی ممنوع ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول کو ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ مسجد کی چھت پر ہمبستری کرنا اور اسی طرح مسجد کی چھت پر پیشاب کرنا جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ کام مسجد میں جائز نہیں ہے اور چونکہ مسجد کی چھت بھی مسجد کے حکم میں ہے لہذا مذکورہ اصول کی بنیاد پر مسجد کی چھت پر بھی یہ کام ممنوع ہیں ، اور مذکورہ اصول ہی کی بنیاد پر اگر امام مسجد کے اندر ہو اور مقتدی نے چھت سے اس امام کی اقتداء کی تو یہ اقتداء جائز ہے، اسی طرح اگر معتکف مسجد کی چھت پر چڑھا تو اس سے اعتکاف باطل نہیں ہو گا، اسی طرح جنبی آدمی کو مسجد کی چھت پر ٹھہر نا جائز نہیں۔
قال محمد رحمه الله وتكره المجامعة والبول فوق المسجد، لأن لسطح المسجد حكم المسجد، وهذا لما عرف أن حكم المسجد ثابت فى الهواء والعرصة جميعاً، ولهذا قلنا: من قام على سطح المسجد مقتدياً بإمام في المسجد، وهو خلف الإمام يجوز، والمعتكف إذا صعد سطح المسجد لا ينتقض اعتكافه، ولا يحل للجنب والحائض والنفساء صعود سطح المسجد، فعلم أن لسطح المسجد حكم المسجد، ثم لا تجوز المجامعة والبول في المسجد، فكذا فوقه
المحيط البرهانی کتاب الاستحسان والكراهية الفصل الخامس، ج ۵ ص ۳۱۷، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
(قَوْلُهُ وَالْبَوَطُئُ فَوْقَهُ وَالْبَوْلُ وَالتَّخَلَّى أَى وَكُرِهَ الْوَطْئُ فَوْقَ الْمَسْجِدِ وَكَذَا الْبَوْلُ وَالتَّغَوُّطُ لأَنَّ سَطْحَ الْمَسْجِدِ لَهُ حُكْمُ الْمَسْجِدِ حَتَّى يَصِحَ الاقْتِدَاء مِنْهُ بِمَنْ تَحْتَهُ وَلَا يَبْطُلُ الاعْتِكَافُ بِالصُّعُودِ إِلَيْهِ وَلَا يَحِلُّ لِلْجُنُبِ الْوُقُوفٌ عَلَيْهِ وَالْمُرَادُ بِالْكَرَاهَةِ كَرَاهَةُ التَّحْرِيمِ.
البحر الرائق: كتاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة، ج ۲ ص ۳۷، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي]
Indeed, the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque. Translation: The roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that whatever ruling applies to the mosque also applies to its roof, i.e., whatever action is permissible in the mosque is permissible on its roof, and whatever action is impermissible in the mosque is also prohibited on its roof. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the mentioned principle, is stating that engaging in intercourse on the mosque roof and similarly urinating on the mosque roof are not permissible because these acts are impermissible in the mosque, and since the mosque roof is also under the ruling of the mosque, therefore based on the mentioned principle these acts are prohibited on the roof. And based on the same principle, if the imam is inside the mosque and a follower follows him from the roof, this following is valid. Similarly, if a mu'takif climbs onto the mosque roof, his i'tikaf is not invalidated. Similarly, it is not permissible for a person in a state of janabah to stay on the mosque roof. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Intercourse and urination on the mosque are disliked (makruh) because the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque. This is because it is known that the ruling of the mosque is established in both the air and the ground. For this reason, we said: Whoever stands on the roof of the mosque following an imam in the mosque, and he is behind the imam, it is permissible. And the mu'takif if he ascends to the roof of the mosque, his i'tikaf is not invalidated. And it is not permissible for a junub, menstruating woman, or woman with postpartum bleeding to ascend to the roof of the mosque. So it is known that the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque. Then intercourse and urination are not permissible in the mosque, so likewise above it. [Al-Muhit al-Burhani, Kitab al-Istihsan wa al-Karahiyya, al-Fasl al-Khamis, vol. 5 p. 317, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyya] (His saying: And intercourse above it, and urination, and relieving oneself, meaning intercourse on the mosque is disliked, and likewise urination and defecation, because the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque, to the extent that following from it of someone below is valid, and i'tikaf is not invalidated by ascending to it, and it is not permissible for a junub to stand on it. And what is meant by dislike is tahrimi dislike (prohibited). [Al-Bahr al-Raiq, Kitab al-Salah, Bab ma yufsid al-salah, vol. 2 p. 37, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami]
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage establishes a fundamental principle of Hanafi fiqh: the roof of a mosque shares the same legal rulings as the mosque itself. The principle is stated concisely in Arabic: 'Inna satha al-masjidi lahu hukmu al-masjid' (Indeed, the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque). The Urdu commentary explains that any action permissible in the mosque is permissible on its roof, and any prohibited action in the mosque is also prohibited on its roof. The author applies this principle to several cases: intercourse and urination on the roof are prohibited because they are prohibited inside the mosque. However, following the imam from the roof is valid because the roof is considered part of the mosque for the purpose of congregation. Similarly, climbing onto the roof does not invalidate i'tikaf (spiritual retreat) because the roof is part of the mosque. Conversely, a person in a state of janabah (major impurity) cannot stay on the roof for the same reason. The passage then quotes Imam Muhammad from Al-Muhit al-Burhani confirming the principle and its applications. It also quotes from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifying that the 'dislike' (karahah) mentioned is karahah tahrimiyyah (prohibited dislike, close to haram). Thus, the ruling is one of prohibition, not mere dislike.

Key Terms
سطح المسجد (sath al-masjid): The roof of the mosque, which is legally considered part of the mosque for rulings.
كراهة التحريم (karahah al-tahrim): A level of prohibition close to haram; an act that is sinful but not proven by a definitive text to be haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which action is permissible on the mosque roof according to the Hanafi school?
A) Urinating
B) Following the imam in prayer
C) Staying in a state of janabah
D) Engaging in intercourse

Key Takeaway
The roof of a mosque is legally identical to its interior: what is allowed inside is allowed on the roof, and what is prohibited inside is prohibited on the roof.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
The Roof of a Mosque Shares Its Rulings
Establishes principle: roof shares mosque rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for prohibited acts on the mosque roof and explain the qiyas reasoning that extends the interior rulings to the roof.

Core Idea
The mosque roof has the same legal rulings as the interior: what is haram inside is also haram on the roof.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi principle: the mosque's rulings extend beyond its walls to its roof. Imam Muhammad establishes that acts prohibited inside the mosque are also prohibited on its roof, using a clear qiyas. We'll explore the evidence and the practical implications for i'tikaf, following the imam, and purity requirements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Pinpoint the specific act being ruled upon (intercourse, urination, defecation) and the hukm: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
Hanafis use 'makruh tahrimi' for acts close to haram.
2
Understand the Principle
Grasp the core principle: 'lisaṭḥil masjidi ḥukmul masjidi' – the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque. This means the sanctity and legal prohibitions of the mosque interior apply to the roof.
3
Examine the Qiyas
Analyze the analogy: the interior is the asl (original case) with forbidden acts, the roof is the far' (new case), and the illa (effective cause) is being part of the mosque structure. The text also provides supporting evidence: following the imam from the roof is valid, i'tikaf is not broken by ascending, and junub/menstruating women may not ascend.
This is a clear qiyas jali with a jaliyyah illa.
4
Apply the Principle to Other Cases
Consider other acts: e.g., is it allowed to eat or sleep on the roof? The principle suggests that acts that are mubah (permissible) inside are also mubah on the roof, as long as no specific prohibition exists.
The roof does not have the same taharah ruling – one does not need wudu to enter the roof, as it is not a dwelling for prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد رحمه الله وتكره المجامعة والبول فوق المسجد، لأن لسطح المسجد حكم المسجد، وهذا لما عرف أن حكم المسجد ثابت فى الهواء والعرصة جميعاً، ولهذا قلنا: من قام على سطح المسجد مقتدياً بإمام في المسجد، وهو خلف الإمام يجوز، والمعتكف إذا صعد سطح المسجد لا ينتقض اعتكافه، ولا يحل للجنب والحائض والنفساء صعود سطح المسجد، فعلم أن لسطح المسجد حكم المسجد، ثم لا تجوز المجامعة والبول في المسجد، فكذا فوقه
المحيط البرهاني كتاب الاستحسان والكراهية الفصل الخامس، ج ٥ ص ٣١٧، ط: دار الكتب العلمية (قَوْلُهُ وَالْبَوْطُئُ فَوْقَهُ وَالْبَوْلُ وَالتَّخَلَّى أَى وَكُرِهَ الْوَطْئُ فَوْقَ الْمَسْجِدِ وَكَذَا الْبَوْلُ وَالتَّغَوُّطُ لأَنَّ سَطْحَ الْمَسْجِدِ لَهُ حُكْمُ الْمَسْجِدِ حَتَّى يَصِحَ الاقْتِدَاء مِنْهُ بِمَنْ تَحْتَهُ وَلَا يَبْطُلُ الاعْتِكَافُ بِالصُّعُودِ إِلَيْهِ وَلَا يَحِلُّ لِلْجُنُبِ الْوُقُوفُ عَلَيْهِ وَالْمُرَادُ بِالْكَرَاهَةِ كَرَاهَةُ التَّحْرِيمِ.
البحر الرائق: كتاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة، ج ٢ ص ٣٧، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي
Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is disliked (makruh) to have intercourse and to urinate on top of the mosque, because the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque. This is because it is known that the ruling of the mosque is established for both the airspace and the ground. For this reason we said: Whoever stands on the roof of the mosque following an imam who is inside the mosque, while he is behind the imam, it is permissible. And if a person in i'tikaf climbs to the roof of the mosque, his i'tikaf is not invalidated. And it is not permissible for a person in major ritual impurity (junub), a menstruating woman, or a woman in postpartum bleeding to ascend to the roof of the mosque. Thus it is known that the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque. Then, since intercourse and urination are not permissible inside the mosque, similarly they are not permissible on top of it. Al-Muhit al-Burhani, Kitab al-Istihsan wal-Karahiyah, al-Fasl al-Khamis, vol. 5, p. 317, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah. (His statement: 'and intercourse on top of it, and urination, and relieving oneself,' meaning: intercourse on top of the mosque is disliked, likewise urination and defecation, because the roof of the mosque has the ruling of the mosque, such that following (the imam) from it by someone below is valid, i'tikaf is not invalidated by ascending to it, and it is not permissible for a junub to stand on it. And what is meant by karahah is karahah tahrimi (prohibited dislike). Al-Bahr al-Raiq, Kitab al-Salah, Bab ma Yufsid al-Salah, vol. 2, p. 37, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, establishing a key fiqh principle: the roof of a mosque shares the same legal rulings as the mosque interior. The author, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, begins by stating that intercourse and urination on the mosque roof are makruh (disliked). This is because the roof has the same ruling as the mosque. The reasoning is that the mosque's rulings apply to both its airspace and its ground area. The passage then gives several applications: 1) Following the imam from the roof is valid if the person is behind the imam. 2) Ascending to the roof does not break i'tikaf. 3) A junub, menstruating woman, or woman in postpartum bleeding may not ascend the roof. This is because entering the mosque for such individuals is forbidden. Finally, it reinforces that since intercourse and urination are forbidden inside the mosque, they are also forbidden on the roof. The source from Al-Muhit al-Burhani clarifies that the karahah here is tahrimi (prohibited), not just disliked. This principle is based on istihsan and qiyas, as the roof is considered an integral part of the mosque.

Key Terms
سطح المسجد (sath al-masjid): The roof of the mosque, considered as an integral part of the mosque for legal rulings.
كراهة التحريم (karahah tahrimi): A dislike that is prohibitively disliked; synonym for haram in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) His i'tikaf is not invalidated.
C) He must leave i'tikaf and start over.
D) It is makruh tanzihi.

Key Takeaway
The roof of a mosque is legally part of the mosque for prohibitions and many rulings, based on a clear qiyas and supporting evidence.

The Imperative Implies Obligation: The Case of Witr Prayer
Proves the principle that the imperative indicates obligation, applied to witr prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi proof that witr is wajib using the imperative form (amr) and the concept of increase (ziyadah), and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
The absolute command (amr) obligates; witr is an increase upon fard, so it too is wajib.

This passage establishes the usul principle that the imperative implies obligation (al-amr lil wujub) and applies it to witr prayer. The Hanafi school argues that witr must be wajib because the Prophet called it an 'increase' (ziyadah) upon the obligatory prayers, and an increase can only be upon something of the same type and quantified. Understanding this reasoning clarifies why Hanafis consider qada of witr obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage begins by stating the usul principle: 'إِنَّ الأمْرَ لِلْوُجُوبِ' (The imperative indicates obligation). Understand what this means.
This is the first step in the proof.
2
Extract the two proofs
The author gives two aspects: (1) the imperative 'sallu' is an amr, so it indicates wujub; (2) the prayer is called a ziyadah (increase) on the fard. Follow the logic of ziyadah.
3
Analyze the ziyadah argument
The passage explains that increase can only be upon something quantified (muqaddar), which is the fard prayer. Nafl is not quantified, so witr cannot be nafl; it must be of the same type (wajib).
This is a key refutation of the sunnah view.
4
Examine the additional supporting evidence
The passage mentions consensus on qada being obligatory, and that the Prophet attributed the increase to Allah (not himself), indicating it is a divine command.
5
Contrast with the opposing view
The Shafi'i school interprets the same hadith as indicating recommendation (nadb). The Hanafi response refutes this by emphasizing the absolute nature of the amr and the ziyadah argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة الوتر
( ١٠٢) إِنَّ الأمْرَ لِلْوُجُوبِ .
والاستدلال به من وجهين: أحدهما أنه أمر بها ومطلق الأمر للوجوب، والثاني أنه سماها زيادة والزيادة على الشيء لا يتصور إلا من جنسه فأما إذا كان غيره فإنه يكون قرانا لا زيادة ولأن الزيادة إنما تتصور على المقدر وهو الفرض، فأما النفل فليس بمقدر فلا تتحقق الزيادة عليه، ولا يقال: إنها زيادة على الفرض لكن في الفعل لا في الوجوب.
بدائع الصنائع: كتاب الصلاة، أنواع صلاة الواجبة ج ١ ص ٢٧١، ط: دار الكتب العلمية والأمر للوجوب ولهذا يجب قضاؤها بالإجماع، ولان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أضاف الزيادة إلى الله لا إلى نفسه والسنن تضاف إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وإنما لم يؤذن لها، لأنها تؤدى في وقت العشاء فاكتفتي بآذانه وإقامته.
الجوهرة النيرة: كتاب الصلاة، باب صفة الصلاة، ج ١ ص ٥٧، ط: المطبعة الخيرية
Chapter of Witr Prayer
(102) The imperative indicates obligation.
And the inference from it is from two aspects: first, that He commanded it, and the absolute imperative indicates obligation. Second, that He called it an increase (ziyadah), and an increase upon something can only be conceived as being of the same type; if it were different, it would be conjunction (qiran), not increase. And because increase is only conceivable upon something quantified, which is the obligatory (fard). As for supererogatory (nafl), it is not quantified, so increase upon it is not realized. It is not said that it is an increase upon the fard in action but not in obligation. From Badai' al-Sanai': Kitab al-Salah, Anwa' al-Salah al-Wajibah, vol. 1, p. 271, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah. And the command indicates obligation, for this reason its make-up (qada) is obligatory by consensus. And because the Prophet (peace be upon him) attributed the increase to Allah, not to himself, and sunan are attributed to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). And it is not given an adhan because it is performed in the time of Isha, so its adhan and iqamah suffice. From Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah: Kitab al-Salah, Bab Sifat al-Salah, vol. 1, p. 57, Al-Matba'ah al-Khayriyyah.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute, establishing the Hanafi position that witr prayer is wajib (obligatory) based on the usul principle that the imperative (amr) indicates obligation. The author from Usool al-Hidayah states this principle and then uses it to prove witr is wajib. The hadith used is: 'Allah has added a prayer for you, which is witr, so pray it between Isha and Fajr.' The inference is two-fold: 1) The verb 'sallu' is an imperative form, and an unqualified imperative implies wujub. 2) The Prophet called witr a 'ziyadah' (increase) upon the fard (obligatory) prayers. An increase can only be upon something of the same kind (i.e., also obligatory) and upon something quantifiable (the five daily prayers are quantified). Supererogatory prayers are not quantified, so this description would not apply. This argument refutes those who say witr is sunnah. The text from Badai' al-Sanai' adds that the consensus (ijma) supports its qada being obligatory, and that the Prophet attributed the increase to Allah himself, not to himself, indicating it is a divine command. The fact that no adhan is called for witr is because it falls within the time of Isha, so its adhan and iqamah serve double duty. This is a classic Hanafi proof for the wujub of witr.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The imperative form (grammatical command) which indicates obligation (wujub) unless there is a context (qarinah) shifting it to recommendation (nadb) or permission (ibahah).
الزيادة (al-ziyadah): An increase upon something existing; here, witr is described as an increase upon the obligatory prayers, implying it must be of their kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi text argue that witr prayer must be wajib based on the hadith calling it a 'ziyadah' (increase)?
A) Because an increase can only be upon something non-obligatory.
B) Because an increase can only be upon something of the same type and quantified, and only the fard prayers are quantified.
C) Because the Prophet himself called it a sunnah.
D) Because it is performed only during Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
The absolute command in the hadith and the description of witr as an increase on fard prove that witr is wajib in the Hanafi school, with qada required.

The Principle 'Al-Amr li al-Wujub' and the Obligation of Witr
Establishing the obligation of Witr via the amr principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that an absolute command (amr mutlaq) indicates obligation (wujub), apply it to the hadith on Witr, and explain why Witr is considered wajib in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
An absolute command indicates obligation — the command 'fisallu' makes Witr wajib.

In this lesson, we explore one of the foundational usul principles of the Hanafi school: that an absolute command (amr mutlaq) conveys obligation (wujub). The author of Al-Hidayah invokes this principle to establish that Witr prayer is wajib according to Imam Abu Hanifa. We will examine the hadith proof, the two lines of deduction, and the intra-school and inter-school implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its definition
State the principle 'al-amr li al-wujub' and define an absolute command as one free from contextual indicators (qara'in) that would shift its meaning.
2
Locate the hadith proof for Witr
Find the hadith in the passage: 'Indeed Allah has added for you a prayer, and it is Witr, so pray it between 'Isha and Fajr.' Identify the command word 'fisallu'.
Hadith from Musnad Ahmad, hadith 23851
3
Apply the principle to the command 'fisallu'
Since the command is absolute (no qarina), according to the principle it indicates wujub. Therefore Witr prayer becomes wajib.
4
Examine the second deduction using the term 'ziyadah'
The Prophet called Witr an 'addition' (ziyadah). Addition can only be to something specified (muqaddar), which is the fard. Thus Witr is a specified addition to the fard prayers, reinforcing its obligatory nature.
Second argument from Al-Hidayah
5
Note the consensus on makeup (qada)
The passage mentions that makeup of Witr is obligatory by consensus, further supporting its wajib status.
From Bada'i' al-Sana'i'

Source Text

باب صلاة الوتر
( ١٠٢) إِنَّ الأمْرَ لِلْوُجُوبِ . ترجمہ: امر (مطلق ) وجوب کے لئے آتا ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ امر مطلق یعنی جو قرائن سے خالی ہو تو وہ وجوب کے
لئے آتا ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ نے مذکورہ اصول کو ذکر کرنے کے بعد اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں امام ابو
حنیفہ رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک وتر واجب ہے، اس لئے کہ آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا: إنَّ اللهَ زَادَكُمْ صَلَاةَ، وَهِيَ الْوِتْرُ فَصَلُّوْهَا فِيْمَا بَيْنَ صَلَاةِ الْعِشَاءِ إِلَى
صَلَاةِ الْفَجْرِ. ترجمہ: بے شک اللہ تعالی نے تمہارے لئے ایک نماز کا اضافہ کیا ہے، وہ نماز نماز وتر ہے،
پس تم اس کو عشاء اور فجر کی نماز کے درمیان پڑھو۔ مذکورہ حدیث میں لفظ ”فَصَلُّوا امر کا صیغہ ہے اور مذکورہ اصول کی بنیاد پر امر وجوب کے لئے آتا ہے، لہذا یہاں بھی فَصَلُّوا وجوب کے لئے ہے، لہذ اوتر کی نماز بھی واجب ہوئی ۔ الهداية: كتاب الصلاة، باب صلاة الوتر ، ج ۱ ص ۱۴۸، ط: رحمانیه
مسند أحمد: ج ۳۹ ص ۲۷۱ ، رقم الحدیث : ۲۳۸۵۱، ط: مؤسسة الرسالة وَالِاسْتِدْلَالُ بِهِ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ : أَحَدُهُمَا أَنَّهُ أَمَرَ بِهَا وَمُطْلَقُ الْأمْرِ لِلْوُجُوبِ، وَالثَّانِي أَنَّهُ سَمَّاهَا زِيَادَةً وَالزِّيَادَةُ عَلَى الشَّيْءِ لَا تُتَصَوَّرُ إِلَّا مِنْ جِنْسِهِ فَأَمَّا إِذَا كَانَ غَيْرَهُ فَإِنَّهُ يَكُونُ قِرَانًا لَا زِيَادَةَ وَلَأَنَّ الزِّيَادَةَ إِنَّمَا تُتَصَوَّرُ عَلَى الْمُقَدَّرِ وَهُوَ الْفَرْضُ، فَأَمَّا النَّفَلُ فَلَيْسَ بِمُقَدَّرٍ فَلَا تَتَحَقَّقُ الزِّيَادَةُ عَلَيْهِ، وَلَا يُقَالُ : إِنَّهَا زِيَادَةٌ عَلَى الْفَرْضِ لَكِنْ فِي الْفِعْلِ لَا فِي الْوُجُوبِ.
بدائع الصنائع: كتاب الصلاة، أنواع صلاة الواجبة ج ۱ ص ۲۷۱، ط: دار الكتب العلمية وَالأمْرُ لِلْوُجُوبِ وَلِهَذَا يَجِبُ قَضَاؤُهَا بِالْإِجْمَاعِ، وَلانَّ النَّبِيَّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ أَضَافَ الزِّيَادَةَ إِلَى اللَّهِ لَا إِلَى نَفْسِهِ وَالسُّنَنُ تُضَافُ إِلَى رَسُولِ اللهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ وَإِنَّمَا لَم يُؤَذِّنُ لَهَا، لِأَنَّهَا تُؤَدَّى فِي وَقْتِ الْعِشَاءِ فَاكْتَفَيْتِ بِآذَانِهِ وَإِقَامَتِهِ.
الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب الصلاة، باب صفة الصلاة ، ج ۱ ص ۵۷، ط: المطبعة الخيرية
Chapter of Witr Prayer. (102) Indeed the command is for obligation. Translation: The (absolute) command comes for obligation. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that the absolute command, i.e., that which is free from contextual indicators, comes for obligation. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the mentioned principle, is proving that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, Witr is obligatory, because the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said: 'Indeed Allah has added for you a prayer, and it is Witr, so pray it between the prayer of 'Isha and the prayer of Fajr.' Translation: Indeed Allah Almighty has added for you a prayer, that prayer is Witr prayer, so pray it between 'Isha and Fajr. In the mentioned hadith, the word 'fisallu' is the form of a command, and based on the mentioned principle, a command comes for obligation, so here too 'fisallu' is for obligation, therefore Witr prayer became obligatory. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Salah, Bab Salat al-Witr, vol. 1 p. 148, Rahmaniyyah edition. Musnad Ahmad: vol. 39 p. 271, hadith no. 23851, Mu'assasat al-Risalah edition. And the deduction from it is from two aspects: one is that He commanded it, and an absolute command is for obligation; the second is that He called it an addition, and addition to something is not conceivable except from its own kind. But if it is other than it, then it would be joining (qiran), not addition. And because addition is only conceivable upon something that is specified (muqaddar), and that is the obligatory (fard). As for the supererogatory (nafl), it is not specified, so addition cannot be realized upon it. And it is not said that it is addition upon the fard, but in terms of the act, not in terms of obligation. Bada'i' al-Sana'i': Kitab al-Salah, Anwa' Salat al-Wajibah, vol. 1 p. 271, Dar al-Kutub al-'Ilmiyyah edition. And the command is for obligation, and for this reason its makeup is obligatory by consensus, and because the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم attributed the addition to Allah, not to himself, and the sunan are attributed to the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم. And he did not call the adhan for it because it is performed in the time of 'Isha, so they were content with its adhan and iqamah. Al-Jawharat al-Nayyirah: Kitab al-Salah, Bab Sifat al-Salah, vol. 1 p. 57, al-Matba'ah al-Khayriyyah edition.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof establishing the Hanafi principle that an absolute command (amr mutlaq) indicates obligation (wujub). The author begins by stating the principle: 'Indeed the command is for obligation.' He explains that 'absolute command' means a command free from contextual indicators (qara'in) that would shift its meaning to recommendation, permissibility, or otherwise. The principle is then applied to the prayer of Witr to prove its obligatory status in the Hanafi school according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The proof is a hadith from Musnad Ahmad where the Prophet said: 'Indeed Allah has added for you a prayer, and it is Witr, so pray it between 'Isha and Fajr.' The word 'fisallu' (so pray) is an imperative (amr), and since the command is absolute, it indicates obligation. The text further cites two deductive arguments from Al-Hidayah: first, that the command itself implies obligation; second, that the prayer is called an 'addition' (ziyadah), and addition can only be to something that is specified (muqaddar), which is the fard (obligatory prayer). Thus Witr is a specified addition to the fard prayers, indicating its obligatory nature. Additional support is given from Bada'i' al-Sana'i' and Al-Jawharat al-Nayyirah, emphasizing that the command indicates obligation and that Witr must be made up if missed, by consensus. The authority weight of this passage is from Zahir al-Riwayah via Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
أَمْر (amr): A command or imperative form; in usul al-fiqh, a speech act demanding an action from a superior to an inferior.
وُجُوب (wujub): Legal obligation; a requirement to perform an act that is not established by qat'i evidence (thus distinguished from fard).
وِتْر (witr): The odd-numbered prayer performed after 'Isha, considered wajib by Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two deductive arguments used to prove Witr is wajib?
A) The command 'fisallu' and the term 'ziyadah' (addition)
B) The command and the fact that it is prayed at night
C) The hadith chain and the consensus
D) The principle and the fact that it is sunnah

Key Takeaway
An absolute command (amr mutlaq) without contextual indicators indicates obligation (wujub), as demonstrated by the hadith on Witr prayer.

The Principle: No Following in Abrogated Actions
Principle and application to Qunut in Fajr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that abrogated rulings cannot be followed and apply it to the case of Qunut in Fajr prayer when following a Shafi'i imam.

Core Idea
An abrogated ruling retains no legal force; following it in prayer is invalid.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle: abrogated (mansukh) rulings cannot be followed in prayer. The author of Al-Hidayah applies this to a common inter-madhhab scenario – a Hanafi follower behind a Shafi'i imam who recites Qunut in Fajr. Understanding this principle helps you navigate differences between schools in congregational prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Read the principle: 'Indeed that which is abrogated has no following in it.' Understand that this is a general rule of Shari'ah: once a ruling is abrogated, it is no longer valid to follow it in action.
The principle is stated concisely, then explained in Urdu commentary.
2
Locate the Applied Case
The principle is applied to a specific scenario: a Shafi'i imam reciting Qunut in Fajr prayer. The Hanafi follower must not recite Qunut or follow the imam in that act.
This is a practical case of ikhtilaf in congregational prayer.
3
Examine the Dalil
The proof is stated: 'And our proof is that it is abrogated because of what we narrated, and there is no following in the abrogated.' This refers to transmitted reports (hadith) that show abrogation of Qunut in Fajr.
The dalil is transmitted (naqli) and supports the principle.
4
Understand the Qiyas
An analogy is made: just as one does not follow an imam who says five takbirs in Janazah prayer (which is not the sunnah), one does not follow an imam in an abrogated action like Qunut in Fajr.
The analogy strengthens the argument by showing consistency.
5
Identify the Thamarah (Practical Outcome)
The practical consequence for a Hanafi follower: if the imam recites Qunut in Fajr, the follower remains silent and does not recite Qunut, nor does he follow the imam in that action. The follower's prayer remains valid.
This is the fatwa (mufta bihi) position.

ترجمہ: جو چیز منسوخ ہو اس میں اقتدار نہیں ہوتی۔

(103) Indeed that which is abrogated has no following in it. Translation: Whatever is abrogated has no authority in it. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that whatever ruling of Shari'ah becomes abrogated, then following (iqtida') is not done in that abrogated ruling. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the mentioned principle, is proving that if a Shafi'i imam recites supplication of Qunut in Fajr prayer, then according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? actually it says 'tarafayn' meaning the two sides? But likely Abu Hanifa and Muhammad or the two shaykhs? The text says 'tarafayn rahimahuma Allah' which might refer to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad? Or it's a typo. In context, it means the Hanafi follower will not recite Qunut, because reciting Qunut in Fajr prayer is now abrogated. Therefore, based on the mentioned principle, it is not correct for the follower to recite Qunut and to follow the imam in Qunut, because there is no following in abrogated things, just as if someone says five takbirs in Janazah, there is no following in that. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Salah, Bab Salat al-Witr, vol. 1 p. 150, Rahmaniyyah edition. And our proof is that it is abrogated because of what we narrated, and there is no following in the abrogated, so it becomes like if he said takbir five times in Janazah where he is not followed. Durar al-Hukkam Sharh Ghurar al-Ahkam: Kitab al-Salah, Bab al-Witr wa al-Nawafil, vol. 1 p. 114, Dar Ihya' al-Kutub edition.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof establishing the principle that abrogated (mansukh) rulings cannot be followed (لا متابعة في المنسوخ). The author begins by stating the principle: 'Indeed that which is abrogated has no following in it.' The commentary explains that when a Shari'ah ruling becomes abrogated, one should not follow (iqtida') that abrogated ruling. This principle is then applied to a practical scenario: if a Shafi'i imam recites the Qunut supplication in Fajr prayer, a Hanafi follower should not recite Qunut nor follow the imam in that action. The reason is that Qunut in Fajr has been abrogated (mansukh) in the Hanafi school. The evidence from Al-Hidayah is: 'And our proof is that it is abrogated because of what we narrated, and there is no following in the abrogated, so it becomes like if he said takbir five times in Janazah where he is not followed.' This is an analogy (qiyas): just as one should not follow an imam who says five takbirs in Janazah prayer (which is not the established sunnah), similarly one should not follow an imam in an abrogated practice like Qunut in Fajr. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah via Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
مَنْسُوخ (mansukh): Abrogated; a legal ruling that has been superseded by a later revelation.
مُتَابَعَة (mutaba'ah): Following or conformity; specifically in prayer, following the imam in actions.
قُنُوت (qunut): A supplication recited in prayer; in this context, the qunut recited in Fajr prayer.

C) Because the follower must remain silent
D) Because the imam is not a Hanafi

Key Takeaway
Abrogated rulings have no legal effect; a Hanafi must not follow an imam in an abrogated action like Qunut in Fajr.

The Principle That a Command Does Not Require Repetition and Its Application to Recitation in Salah
Principle statement with dispute on recitation in the second rak'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that a command does not necessitate repetition, and apply it to the dispute about recitation in the second rak'ah, distinguishing between ibarat al-nass and dalalat al-nass.

Core Idea
A single command obligates once; repetition requires additional evidence like textual indication or analogy.

In this lesson, we encounter a key usul principle: al-amr bi al-fi'l la yaqtadi al-tikrar. The author of al-Hidayah uses it to frame a dispute with Hasan al-Basri about the number of rak'ahs in which recitation is obligatory. The Hanafi response illustrates how a general principle can coexist with an extension of the ruling via textual indication (dalalat al-nass).

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Understand the meaning of 'a command to do an act does not require repetition' — the commanded act is fulfilled once; repeating it is not automatically required.
This principle is a Hanafi usul rule about the nature of commands.
2
Identify the dispute
Hasan al-Basri argues that based on the Quranic command 'faqra'u', recitation is obligatory only in one rak'ah because the command does not repeat.
He relies on the verse and the principle directly.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
Hanafis accept the principle but claim that the obligation in the second rak'ah comes from dalalat al-nass (textual indication) rather than from ibarat al-nass (explicit text).
The response does not break the principle; it uses a different source of obligation.
4
Examine the analogy (qiyas)
The second rak'ah is completely similar (tashakul min kulli wajh) to the first in obligation, dropping, quality, and quantity. This complete similarity allows the ruling to be extended by inference.
Qiyas jaliy is used here because the illa is evident.
5
Distinguish ibarat al-nass and dalalat al-nass
Ibarat al-nass is the direct textual meaning; dalalat al-nass is an inferred indication from the text's context or analogy. The Hanafi position relies on the latter for the second rak'ah.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النوافل
(۱۰۴) إِنَّ الْأمْرَ بِالْفِعْلِ لَا يَقْتَضِى التَّكْرَارَ . ترجمہ: امر بالفعل تکرار کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کسی فعل کا حکم کرنا تکرار نہیں چاہتا، یعنی مامور پر وہ کام ایک مرتبہ واجب ہے دوبارہ کرنا واجب نہیں ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول کو ذکر کرنے کے بعد اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ حسن بصری رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک فرض نماز میں صرف ایک رکعت میں قرات واجب ہے، کیونکہ قرآن کریم میں اللہ تعالی ارشاد فرماتے ہیں: فَاقْرَؤُوا مَا تَيَسَّرَ مِنَ الْقُرآن . (المزمل: ۲۰) قرآن میں جو آسان لگے اسی کو نماز میں پڑھو۔ لہذا اس آیت میں فَاقْرَؤُوا امر ہے اور امر تکرار کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا ہے، تو مذکورہ اصول کی بنیاد پر حسن بصری رحمہ اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ صرف ایک رکعت میں قراءت واجب ہے۔ ائمہ احناف اس کے جواب میں کہتے ہیں کہ ہم بھی اس اصول کو تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ امر تکرار نہیں چاہتا تو اس کی بنیاد پر صرف ایک ہی رکعت میں قرات واجب ہے لیکن دوسری رکعت میں ہم جو وجوب قرات کے قائل ہیں وہ اس وجہ سے کہ دوسری رکعت چونکہ پہلی رکعت کے ساتھ من کل الوجوہ مشابہ ہے، تو اسی مشابہت کی بنیاد پر ہم نے کہا کہ دوسری رکعت میں بھی قراءت واجب ہے۔ پس پہلی رکعت میں قراءت عبارت النص سے ثابت ہے اور دوسری رکعت میں دلالت النص سے ثابت ہے۔ ولنا قَوْله تَعَالَى: فَاقْرَءُوا مَا تَيَسَّرَ مِنْهُ) والأمر بالفعل لا يقتضي التكرار) تقديره أن الله تعالى أمرنا بالقراءة مما تيسر من القرآن وذلك فى الصلاة بالإجماع والأمر بالفعل يقتضى امتثاله، ولا يقتضي التكرار إعادة الشيء بعينه لا إعادة مثل الشيء فاقتضى ذلك أن تكون القراءة في ركعة واحدة كما ذهب إليه الحسن البصرى. وإنما أوجبنا في الثانية) أى إنما أوجبنا القراءة في الركعة الثانية، وهذا جواب عما يقال إنكم قلتم إن الأمر بالفعل لا يقتضى التكرار، وقد أوجبتم القراءة فى الركعة الثانية وخالفتم ما قلتم وتقرير الجواب أن وجوب القراءة في الثانية لا بعبارة النص حتى يلزم ما قلتم وإنما وجوبها في الثانية بدلالة النص وهو معنى قوله (استدلالا) بالأولى يعنى بالركعة الأولى وبين ذلك بقوله : (لأنهما يتشاكلان من كل وجه أى لأن الركعة الأولى والثانية تتشابهان من كل وجه، فلما كان كذلك وجبت في الثانية استدلالا بالأولى كالحكم في أحد النوعين ينسب في النوع الآخر، وأما تشاكل الثانية للأولى من كل وجه فمن حيث السقوط والوجوب والصفة والقدر فكل من وجبت عليه الأولى وجبت الثانية، وإذا سقطت سقطت . وأما المماثلة في الصفة ففى الجهر والأخير الإخفاء ، وأما المماثلة في القدر ففى ضم السورة مع الفاتحة.
البناية: كتاب الصلاة، باب النوافل، فصل فى القراءة، ج ۲ ص ۵۲۵، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
Chapter of Supererogatory Prayers
(104) Indeed, a command to do an act does not necessitate repetition. Translation: A command to do an act does not require repetition.
Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that commanding an action does not require repetition, i.e., the commanded person is obligated to do it once; doing it again is not obligatory. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, is proving that according to Hasan al-Basri (may Allah have mercy on him), recitation in the obligatory prayer is obligatory in only one rak'ah, because Allah Almighty says in the Quran: 'So recite what is easy from the Quran' (al-Muzzammil 20). So recite from the Quran what is easy in prayer. Therefore, in this verse, 'faqra'u' is a command, and a command does not require repetition. So based on the mentioned principle, Hasan al-Basri (may Allah have mercy on him) says that recitation is obligatory in only one rak'ah. The Hanafi imams, in response, say: We also accept this principle that a command does not require repetition, so based on it, recitation is obligatory in only one rak'ah. But what we hold regarding the obligation of recitation in the second rak'ah is because the second rak'ah is completely similar to the first rak'ah in all aspects. On the basis of this similarity, we say that recitation is obligatory in the second rak'ah as well. Thus, recitation in the first rak'ah is proven by the textual expression (ibarat al-nass), and in the second rak'ah by textual indication (dalalat al-nass). And we have the saying of Allah Almighty: 'So recite what is easy from it' and the principle 'a command to do an act does not necessitate repetition', meaning that Allah Almighty commanded us to recite what is easy from the Quran, and that is in prayer by consensus. A command to do an act requires its compliance, and does not require repetition of the same thing itself, not repetition of the like of the thing. So this necessitated that recitation be in one rak'ah as Hasan al-Basri held. As for our obligation in the second (i.e., we obligated recitation in the second rak'ah), this is a response to what is said: You said that a command to do an act does not necessitate repetition, yet you obligated recitation in the second rak'ah, contradicting what you said. The explanation of the response is that the obligation of recitation in the second is not by the expression of the text (ibarat al-nass) so that what you said would apply; rather, its obligation in the second is by textual indication (dalalat al-nass), which is the meaning of his saying 'by inference (istidlalan) from the first', meaning from the first rak'ah. He clarified that by saying: 'Because they resemble each other in every respect', i.e., because the first and second rak'ahs resemble each other in every respect. So when that is the case, it becomes obligatory in the second by inference from the first, just as the ruling of one type is applied to the other type. As for their resemblance in every respect: in terms of obligation, drop, quality, and quantity. For whoever the first becomes obligatory, the second becomes obligatory; and if it drops, it drops. As for similarity in quality: in loud and quiet recitation. As for similarity in quantity: in adding a surah to the Fatiha.
Al-Binayah: Book of Prayer, Chapter of Nawafil, Section on Recitation, vol. 2 p. 525, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage introduces another key principle: 'A command to do an act does not necessitate repetition' (al-amr bi al-fi'l la yaqtadi al-tikrar). The commentator explains that the principle means that when Allah commands an act, it is fulfilled once; repetition is not required. This is applied to a dispute with Hasan al-Basri regarding recitation (qira'ah) in the obligatory prayer. Hasan al-Basri argues that based on the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' (recite what is easy from the Quran), which is a command, recitation is obligatory only in one rak'ah because the command does not repeat. The Hanafi school responds by accepting the principle but explaining that the obligation of recitation in the second rak'ah comes from dalalat al-nass (textual indication) rather than ibarat al-nass (textual expression). Because the two rak'ahs are completely analogous in all respects (obligation, dropping, quality, quantity), the obligation established for the first rak'ah by the explicit text is extended to the second rak'ah via inference (istidlal). This illustrates how Hanafi usul al-fiqh uses both explicit and implicit textual evidence to derive rulings, and how a general principle can be qualified by another legal mechanism.

Key Terms
الأمر بالفعل (al-amr bi al-fi‘l): A command from the Lawgiver to perform a specific action.
التكرار (al-tikrār): Repetition of the same act multiple times.
عبارة النص (ibārat al-nass): The explicit wording of the text from which a ruling is directly derived.
دلالة النص (dalālat al-nass): An indication from the text that is not explicit but inferred through meaning or context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do Hanafis justify the obligation of recitation in the second rak'ah despite the principle that a command does not require repetition?
A) They reject the principle entirely.
B) They use a different verse that explicitly commands recitation in every rak'ah.
C) They claim that the second rak'ah is obligatory by textual indication (dalalat al-nass) due to its similarity with the first.
D) They hold that the principle applies only to supererogatory prayers.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school demonstrates that a principle (al-amr la yaqtadi al-tikrar) can be preserved while extending a ruling through textual indication (dalalat al-nass) when complete similarity exists.

Obligation of Recitation in the Second Rak'ah: Hanafi Response to Hasan al-Basri
Hanafi response to Hasan al-Basri on qira'ah obligation in second rak'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on recitation in the second rak'ah of fard prayer and explain the usul basis using ibarat al-nass and dalaalat al-nass.

Core Idea
Recitation is wajib in both rak'ahs; the second is obligated by implication (dalaalat al-nass) not repetition.

A key usul principle — al-amr la yufidu al-takraar — seems to limit the obligation of recitation to one rak'ah. Hasan al-Basri relied on this. The Hanafi school, however, argues that the second rak'ah is not a repetition of the first but a similar act whose obligation is derived by dalaalat al-nass, a form of qiyas jaliy. This lesson walks through that argument and its foundation in complete resemblance between rak'ahs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school holds: recitation (qira'ah) is wajib in every rak'ah of fard prayer, including the second.
This is the mufta bihi position with no intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Hasan al-Basri holds that recitation is wajib only once. His proof: the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara' is an amr, and amr does not require repetition (takraar).
The principle 'al-amr la yufidu al-takraar' is accepted by Hanafis as well.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis concede the principle but distinguish: the first rak'ah is wajib by ibarat al-nass (explicit wording). The second is wajib by dalaalat al-nass because the two rak'ahs resemble each other in every respect (obligation, description, quantity).
Thus no contradiction: the command does not repeat the same act; it obligates a similar act via inference.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafis require recitation in both rak'ahs; omitting it in the second may invalidate the prayer. Hasan al-Basri's view would allow omitting it in the second.
The Hanafi ruling is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
